[4:0:344,450] Legislative, Judicial, and Executive section of the  

[4:1:344,460] Governor's Budget are either established as  

[4:2:344,469] independent entities under the California  

[5:0:344,479] Constitution, or are departments with a recognized  

[5:1:344,489] need to operate outside of the administrative  

[5:2:344,499] oversight and control of an agency secretary.  

[5:3:344,509] Constitutionally-established bodies include the  

[5:4:344,519] Legislature, Judicial Branch, Governor's Office,  

[5:5:344,529] and constitutional officers. This section also  

[5:6:344,539] includes such independent entities as the  

[6:0:344,549] Inspector General and the California State Lottery.

[1:0:268,53] 0110   Senate  

[2:0:66,75] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[2:1:66,85] establishing public policy for California. The State Senate is comprised of 40 members who are elected for 4-year terms.  

[2:2:66,95] Members of the Legislature may serve a maximum of 12 years total in the Senate, the Assembly, or both, in any combination of  

[2:3:66,105] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more  

[2:4:66,116] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[12:0:243,611] 0130   Joint Expenses  

[13:0:66,633] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[13:1:66,643] establishing public policy for California. Both houses contribute to support the Legislative Analyst's Office, which provides fiscal  

[13:2:66,653] and policy advice to the Legislature, and is overseen by the 16-member bipartisan Joint Legislative Budget Committee.  

[13:3:66,671] Because the Legislature's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Legislature has a capital outlay program to  

[13:4:66,681] support this need.   For the specifics on the Legislature's   capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[11:0:66,477] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[11:1:66,496] The California State Capitol, which serves as the state’s working seat of government, began construction in 1860 and was  

[11:2:66,506] completed in 1874. The Capitol remained largely unchanged until 1949, when additional space requirements drove the need for  

[11:3:66,516] construction of the Capitol Annex, which attaches to the east side of the original Capitol building.   The Annex, which was  

[11:4:66,526] completed in 1951, houses legislative offices, committee rooms, and the Governor’s offices.  

[11:5:66,544] Government Code Section 9112(a) authorizes the Joint Rules Committee to pursue the construction of a new, restored,  

[11:6:66,554] rehabilitated, renovated, or reconstructed capitol building annex and associated projects.

[7:0:139,276] 0150   Contributions to the Legislators' Retirement System  

[7:1:66,297] Established in 1947, the Legislators' Retirement System provides retirement and death benefits for legislators, constitutional  

[7:2:66,307] officers, and legislative statutory officers.  

[8:0:66,328] The California Public Employees' Retirement System (CalPERS) administers the Legislators' Retirement System on behalf of  

[8:1:66,338] its members. Retirement benefits paid are based upon age, years of service, and final compensation. Benefits for death,  

[8:2:66,348] disability, and surviving dependents of deceased members are also provided under specific conditions.   The Legislators'  

[8:3:66,358] Retirement System is funded through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment earnings. 

[9:0:66,379] Proposition 140, approved by voters in 1990, closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new legislators after November  

[9:1:66,389] 6, 1990.   Subsequently, the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 2012 (AB 340)  

[9:2:66,399] closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new members effective January 1, 2013. New constitutional officers, statutory  

[9:3:66,409] officers, Sergeant-at-Arms, and the Insurance Commissioner retain optional membership rights in CalPERS.

[6:0:208,190] 0160   Legislative Counsel Bureau  

[6:1:66,211] The Legislative Counsel Bureau (Bureau) provides legal assistance to the two houses of the Legislature, and their members  

[6:2:66,222] and committees, by resolving a large volume of complex legal problems arising in connection with the legislative process. The  

[6:3:66,232] legal services furnished include rendering opinions, drafting bills, counseling, attending as counsel meetings of legislative  

[6:4:66,242] committees, and representing the Legislature in litigation. The attorney-client relationship is maintained and all work is  

[6:5:66,252] confidential.  

[7:0:66,273] In addition, the Bureau prepares and provides necessary indices and appropriate tables necessary to identify legislative  

[7:1:66,283] measures and compiles and indexes statutes and codes.  

[8:0:66,303] The Bureau operates the Legislative Data Center, which provides information technology services in support of the legislative  

[8:1:66,313] information system and the processing of legislative measures.  

[9:0:66,334] Pursuant to Chapter 46 of the Statutes of 2012, the California Commission on Uniform State Laws was transferred into the  

[9:1:66,344] Bureau. The California Commission on Uniform State Laws presents to the Legislature uniform laws recommended by the  

[9:2:66,354] National Conference of Commissioners on Uniform State Laws and then promotes passage of these uniform acts.  

[9:3:66,372] Pursuant to Chapter 450 of the Statutes of 2018, the Bureau provides advice and investigation services to the Legislature  

[9:4:66,382] related to workplace misconduct through the Workplace Conduct Unit.

[9:0:242,495] 0250   Judicial Branch  

[10:0:66,516] Article VI of the Constitution creates the Supreme Court of California and the Courts of Appeal to exercise the judicial power of  

[10:1:66,527] the state at the appellate level. Article VI also creates the Judicial Council of California to administer the state's judicial system.  

[10:2:66,537] Chapter 869, Statutes of 1997, created the California Habeas Corpus Resource Center to represent any person financially  

[10:3:66,547] unable to employ appellate counsel in capital cases.  

[11:0:66,567] The Lockyer-Isenberg Trial Court Funding Act of 1997 (Chapter 850, Statutes of 1997) provided a stable and consistent  

[11:1:66,578] funding source for the trial courts. Beginning with fiscal year 1997-98, consolidation of the costs of trial court operation was  

[11:2:66,588] implemented at the state level, with the exception of facility, revenue collection, and local judicial benefit costs. This  

[12:0:66,598] implementation capped the counties' general purpose revenue contributions to trial court costs at a revised 1994-95 level. The  

[12:1:66,608] county contributions become part of the Trial Court Trust Fund, which supports all trial court operations. Fine and penalty  

[13:0:66,618] revenue collected by each county is retained or distributed in accordance with statute. Each county makes quarterly payments  

[13:1:66,629] to the Trial Court Trust Fund equal to the fine and penalty revenue received by the state General Fund in 1994-95, as adjusted  

[13:2:66,639] by amounts equivalent to specified fine and fee revenues that counties benefited from in 2003-04. The Trial Court Facilities Act  

[13:3:66,649] of 2002 (Chapter 1082, statutes of 2002) provided a process for the responsibility for court facilities to be transferred from the  

[13:4:66,659] counties to the state by July 1, 2007, which was extended to December 31, 2009, by Chapter 9, Statutes of 2008. The Trial  

[13:5:66,669] Court Facilities Act of 2002 also established several new revenue sources, effective on January 1, 2003. These revenues are  

[13:6:66,680] deposited into the State Court Facilities Construction Fund for the purpose of funding the construction and maintenance of  

[14:0:66,690] court facilities throughout the state. Counties contribute revenues for the ongoing operation and maintenance of court facilities  

[14:1:66,700] based upon historical expenditures for facilities transferred to the state.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:207,29] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,83] The mission of the Judicial Branch is to resolve disputes arising under the law and to interpret and apply the law consistently,  

[3:1:66,93] impartially, and independently to protect the rights and liberties guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and the United  

[3:2:66,103] States, in a fair, accessible, effective, and efficient manner.  

[4:0:66,124] Because the Judicial Branch's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Judicial Branch has a capital outlay  

[5:0:66,134] program to support this need. For   the specifics on   the Judicial Branch's capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[3:0:66,102] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[3:1:65,122] • Trial Court Trust Fund Shortfall—The Budget includes an additional $238.5 million one-time General Fund in 2020-21 to  

[3:2:76,132] backfill declining fine and fee revenues: $90.9 million in 2019-20 and $147.6 million for 2020-21.  

[3:3:65,148] • Judicial Branch Budget Reductions—The Budget includes baseline reductions of $176.9 million General Fund for the trial  

[3:4:76,158] courts and $23.1 million General Fund for the state level judiciary in 2020-21. However, If the federal government provides  

[3:5:76,168] sufficient funding by October 15, 2020, $126.9 million for the trial courts and $23.1 million for the state level judiciary will be  

[3:6:76,179] restored in 2020-21.  

[4:0:65,195] • Resources to Fund Additional Workload from COVID-19—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund in  

[4:1:76,205] 2020-21 to help the trial courts address the backlog in cases stemming from COVID-19 and resume normal operations.  

[4:2:65,221] • Trial Court Employee Benefits—The Budget includes $30 million ongoing General Fund for trial court employee health  

[4:3:76,231] benefit and retirement costs, which brings the total funding for this purpose to $180.5 million.  

[4:4:65,248] • Modernization of Court Operations—The Budget includes $25 million General Fund in 2020-21 and 2021-22 for modernizing  

[4:5:76,258] court operations with the goal of achieving efficiencies and increasing access to court services online. The Judicial Council  

[4:6:76,268] may allocate this funding for projects such as enabling electronic case filing, digitizing documents, resolving disputes online,  

[4:7:76,278] and utilizing video technology to facilitate remote participating in hearings.  

[5:0:65,294] • Language Access—The Budget includes $9.9 million General Fund in 2020-21 and $9.6 million annually thereafter for  

[5:1:76,305] increased costs for court interpreters and to purchase equipment for the newly established Video Remote Interpreting  

[5:2:76,315] Program. This will bring total funding for court interpreters to $132 million annually.  

[5:3:65,331] • County Law Libraries Backfill—The Budget includes $7 million one-time General Fund in 2020-21 to backfill County Law  

[5:4:76,341] Libraries for lost revenue due to the reduction in civil case filings from COVID-19.  

[5:5:65,357] • Litigation Management Program—The Budget includes $437,000 General Fund in 2020-21 and annually thereafter for the  

[5:6:76,368] Judicial Branch to pay for legal defense services provided by the Department of Justice.

[16:0:38,359] 1/ Includes all Program 0150 "State Trial Court Funding" expenses from all funds, including the Trial Court Trust Fund (TCTF), State Trial Court Improvement and Modernization Fund (IMF), General Fund (GF), and Judicial Branch Workers'  

[16:1:38,367] Compensation Fund (JBWCF). The expenses do not necessarily represent all expenses for those programs have a state operations component (e.g., Equal Access Fund) and/or a distribution that is made directly to courts (e.g., court-appointed  

[16:2:38,375] dependency counsel). 

[17:0:38,383] 2/ These expenses are funded by the trial courts opting to participate in those programs from their "State Funding" revenue distribution amounts reported in Section 1 of the "State and Non-State Trial Court Funding, Expenditures, and Positions"  

[17:1:38,392] display. Because the funding for these expenses is already included in Section 1, they are subtracted from the total "State Trial Court Funding Not Distributed to the Trial Courts" amount used to calculate the total expenditures displayed for "State  

[17:2:38,399] Trial Court Funding Total".  

[18:0:38,415] 3/ This reflects judges' compensation paid excluding any amounts related to the reimbursement of courts for payments of judges' compensation as these amounts are already included in the courts' "State Funding" revenue amounts reported in the  

[18:1:38,423] Trial Court Information section ($131.2 million in 2018-19 and $137.1 million in 2019-20). Judges from the Los Angeles, Riverside, and Ventura County Superior Courts are compensated locally and the court/county is reimbursed by the TCTF. In  

[18:2:38,430] addition, 23 courts in 2018-19 and 2019-20 participate in the local salary reimbursement program in which a small portion of the judges' salary is paid locally and reimbursed by the TCTF.
[19:0:38,440] 4/ Reflects actual or estimated payments made directly to court-appointed dependency counsel from the TCTF on behalf of superior courts participating in the Dependency Representation, Administration, Funding, and Training (DRAFT) Program.  

[19:1:38,448] Courts not participating in the DRAFT program are reimbursed up to a maximum amount from the TCTF for payments to court-appointed dependency counsel. 

[20:0:38,457] 5/ For the TCTF, expenses are the annual premiums for 57 participating trial courts from their allocations and for superior court judges from the appropriation for the Compensation of Superior Court Judges that are transferred to the JBWCF. For  

[20:1:38,466] the JBWCF, these expenses reflect payments of trial court employee and judge related workers' compensation claims from the JBWCF less the amount transferred from the TCTF.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:414,13] 2020-21 STATE BUDGET — LJE   23 

[2:0:207,29] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,53] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,72] 0130 - SUPREME COURT  

[4:0:66,93] The Supreme Court is the highest court in the California judicial system. Its decisions are binding on all other California state  

[4:1:66,103] courts. The Chief Justice of California and the six Associate Justices entertain petitions seeking review of decisions from the  

[4:2:66,113] Courts of Appeal, original petitions for extraordinary relief (such as writs of mandate or habeas corpus), and recommendations  

[4:3:66,123] for discipline of judicial officers and attorneys. The Court grants review and issues opinions in order to settle legal questions of  

[4:4:66,134] statewide importance. In addition, under the California Constitution, all death penalty judgments are appealed directly to the  

[4:5:66,144] Supreme Court.  

[4:6:66,161] 0135 - COURTS OF APPEAL  

[5:0:66,182] Established by a constitutional amendment in 1904, the Courts of Appeal are California's intermediate courts of review. The six  

[5:1:66,192] District Courts of Appeal hear appeals and original proceedings at nine different locations around the state. Cases before the  

[5:2:66,202] Courts of Appeal involve every area of civil and criminal law.  

[5:3:66,220] 0140 - JUDICIAL COUNCIL  

[6:0:66,240] The Judicial Council of California is the constitutional policy-making body for the state judiciary. The Council consists of 21  

[6:1:66,251] voting members and 10 advisory members; the Chief Justice of California serves as chair. The Judicial Council staff serve as  

[6:2:66,261] the administrative arm of the Council. Staff provide policy support to the Council, administrative accountability in the operation  

[6:3:66,271] of the courts as specified by law, strategic planning for capital outlay, design, and construction of court facilities; and  

[6:4:66,281] administrative support for courts in areas such as budget, fiscal services, coordination of the assignment of retired judges,  

[6:5:66,291] technology, education, legal advice and services, human resources, legislative advocacy, and research.  

[6:6:66,309] 0140023 - JUDICIAL BRANCH FACILITY PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,327] The Judicial Branch Facility Program administers the acquisition, planning, construction, operations, and maintenance of  

[7:1:66,337] judicial branch facilities. This program is responsible for the development of long-term facilities master plans, facility and real  

[7:2:66,347] estate management, renovation of existing court facilities, and new courthouse planning, design, and construction.  

[7:3:66,365] 0150 - STATE TRIAL COURT FUNDING  

[7:4:66,383] 0150010 - SUPPORT FOR THE OPERATION OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[8:0:66,400] This program's objective is to provide the resources necessary for the statewide trial court system to adjudicate civil and  

[8:1:66,410] criminal cases. This program includes all allowable trial court administrative costs under Government Code section 77003,  

[8:2:66,421] except court-appointed dependency counsel, salaries and benefits of Superior Court judges, compensation for assigned  

[8:3:66,431] judges, support for language interpreters, grants, and expenses on behalf of the trial courts.  

[8:4:66,449] 0150011 - COURT APPOINTED DEPENDENCY COUNSEL  

[9:0:66,466] This program provides funding for court-appointed counsel for children, non-minor dependents and parents in juvenile court  

[9:1:66,476] dependency proceedings pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 317, subdivisions (b) and (c), and Government  

[9:2:66,487] Code section 77003, subdivision (a)(4).  

[9:3:66,504] 0150019 - COMPENSATION OF SUPERIOR COURT JUDGES  

[9:4:66,522] This program provides funding for the salaries and state benefits for Superior Court judges.  

[9:5:66,540] 0150028 - ASSIGNED JUDGES  

[10:0:66,557] This program provides support for the salaries and related costs of retired as well as active judges who are assigned by the  

[10:1:66,568] Chief Justice to positions in courts which require assistance due to judicial absences or other factors impacting the ability of a  

[10:2:66,578] court to avoid case delay.  

[10:3:66,596] 0150037 - COURT INTERPRETERS  

[11:0:66,613] This program supports the provision of qualified language interpreters in criminal, juvenile, family and civil proceedings as  

[11:1:66,623] required by statute.  

[11:2:66,641] 0150046 - GRANTS  

[12:0:66,659] This program provides funding for government grants providing public services related to the trial courts.  

[12:1:66,677] 0150095 - EXPENSES ON BEHALF OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[13:0:66,694] This program provides appropriation for statewide programs and services provided to the court or courts by the Judicial Council  

[13:1:66,704] or payment for services or property of any kind contracted for the court or courts or on behalf of the courts by the Judicial  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,83] Council, which are paid for or reimbursed by the trial courts. The appropriation also supports the implementation and  

[3:1:66,93] administration of the Civil Representation Pilot Program.  

[3:2:66,111] 0155 - HABEAS CORPUS RESOURCE CENTER  

[4:0:66,131] The Habeas Corpus Resource Center provides legal representation for indigent petitioners in death penalty habeas corpus  

[4:1:66,141] proceedings before the California state courts   and the federal courts. The Center also recruits and trains attorneys to expand  

[4:2:66,152] the pool of private counsel qualified to accept appointments in death penalty habeas corpus proceedings, serves as a resource  

[4:3:66,162] to them, and thereby helps to reduce the number of unrepresented indigents on California's death row.

[3:0:66,469] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[3:1:66,488] The Judicial Branch consists of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, trial courts, and the Judicial Council. The Supreme Court  

[3:2:66,498] is located in the 101,500 square foot (sf) Civic Center Plaza in San Francisco and the 7,600 sf   Ronald Reagan State Building in  

[3:3:66,509] Los Angeles. The courts of appeal are organized into six districts, operate in nine different locations, and consist of  

[3:4:66,519] approximately 508,000 sf. The trial courts are located in 58 counties statewide, in approximately 450 facilities, and 2,100  

[3:5:66,529] courtrooms of approximately 16 million sf of usable area and more than 21 million sf of space under Judicial Council  

[3:6:66,539] responsibility and management. The facilities of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, and trial courts encompass not only the  

[3:7:66,549] public courtroom spaces but also the chambers and workspaces where judicial officers and courtroom staff prepare for  

[3:8:66,560] proceedings; secure areas, including holding cells; and building support functions. Judicial Council administrative facilities,  

[3:9:66,570] occupying approximately 270,000 sf, are located in San Francisco and Sacramento.

[6:0:180,277] 0280   Commission on Judicial Performance  

[7:0:66,298] The California Commission on Judicial Performance is the independent state agency responsible for investigating complaints of  

[7:1:66,308] judicial misconduct and judicial incapacity, and for disciplining judges pursuant to Article VI, Section 18 of the California  

[7:2:66,318] Constitution. It is the only body with such authority in the state. Its jurisdiction includes all active judges and justices of  

[7:3:66,329] California's superior courts, Courts of Appeal and Supreme Court, and former judges for conduct prior to retirement or  

[7:4:66,339] resignation. The Commission also shares authority with the local courts for the oversight of court commissioners and referees.  

[7:5:66,349] In addition to its disciplinary functions, the Commission is responsible for handling judges' applications for disability retirement.  

[8:0:66,369] The Commission's authority is limited to investigating alleged judicial misconduct and, if warranted, imposing discipline. Judicial  

[8:1:66,380] misconduct usually involves conduct in conflict with the standards set forth in the Code of Judicial Ethics. After investigation  

[8:2:66,390] and, in some cases a public hearing, the Commission may impose sanctions ranging from confidential discipline to removal  

[8:3:66,400] from office.  

[9:0:66,420] The Commission is composed of 11 members: 3 judges appointed by the Supreme Court; 2 attorneys appointed by the  

[9:1:66,431] Governor; and 6 lay citizens, of which 2 are appointed by the Governor, 2 are appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules,  

[9:2:66,441] and 2 are appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. Members are appointed to four-year terms and may serve two terms.  

[9:3:66,451] Commission members do not receive a salary.

[1:0:150,83] 0390   Contributions to the Judges' Retirement System  

[2:0:66,105] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement benefit funding for judges on California's Supreme, Appellate, and  

[2:1:66,115] Superior/Municipal courts. The California Public Employees' Retirement System administers both the Judges' Retirement  

[2:2:66,125] System and the Judges' Retirement System II on behalf of its members.  

[3:0:66,143] The Judges' Retirement System is closed to new members, and a second retirement system for judges was established in  

[3:1:66,153] 1994. All new judges elected or appointed on or after November 9, 1994 become members of the Judges' Retirement System  

[3:2:66,163] II. The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, final  

[3:3:66,173] compensation, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement System II Law. Judges' Retirement  

[3:4:66,184] System II members eligible for a service retirement have the option of choosing the monetary credit plan (a lump-sum return of  

[3:5:66,194] contributions and interest earned) or the defined benefit plan. Judges' Retirement System II members not eligible for a defined  

[3:6:66,204] benefit receive the balance of their monetary credits (in a lump-sum payment). The Judges' Retirement System II receives  

[3:7:66,214] contributions from judges equal to 8 percent of salary as well as any investment income. The state's contributions are adjusted  

[3:8:66,224] annually to maintain actuarial soundness of the fund.  

[4:0:66,245] The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service,  

[4:1:66,255] compensation of active judges, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement Law. Active judges  

[4:2:66,265] and the state each contribute 8 percent of salary to the Judges' Retirement System. Additional contributions come from filing  

[4:3:66,275] fees for specific civil cases and investment income. These contributions, however, are not sufficient to fully fund benefit  

[4:4:66,286] payments. Consequently, current law requires the state to fund the difference between existing contribution sources and the  

[4:5:66,296] required benefit payments to retired judges.  

[5:0:66,316] Effective January 1, 2013, provisions of the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of  

[5:1:66,326] 2012 (AB 340), require that new judges contribute 50 percent of the normal cost of their pension benefits.

[5:0:66,316] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,336] 0220 - GO-Biz  

[6:0:66,356] The GO-Biz serves as the Governor's lead entity for economic strategy and the marketing of California on issues relating to  

[6:1:66,366] business development, private sector investment, and economic growth, and export promotion. This program makes  

[6:2:66,376] recommendations to the Governor and the Legislature regarding policies, programs, and actions to advance statewide  

[6:3:66,387] economic goals.  

[7:0:66,404] 0225 - CALIFORNIA BUSINESS INVESTMENT SERVICES  

[8:0:66,422] This program serves employers, corporate executives, business owners, and site location consultants who are considering  

[8:1:66,432] California for business investment and expansion. This program convenes teams on key business development issues. This  

[8:2:66,442] program works with local, state, and federal partners to attract, retain, and grow businesses in addition to providing permit  

[8:3:66,453] assistance and helping businesses succeed in California. The Innovation Hub (iHub) initiative improves the state's national and  

[8:4:66,463] global competitiveness by stimulating partnerships, economic development, and job creation around specific research clusters  

[8:5:66,473] through state-designated iHubs.  

[9:0:66,491] 0230 - OFFICE OF THE SMALL BUSINESS ADVOCATE  

[10:0:66,508] The director of the Office of the Small Business Advocate (OSBA) serves as the principal advocate in the state on behalf of  

[10:1:66,519] small businesses, including legislation and administrative regulations that affect small business. The OSBA is responsible for  

[10:2:66,529] disseminating information about programs and services provided by the state that benefit small businesses, and how small  

[10:3:66,539] businesses can participate in these programs and services. The OSBA responds to issues from small businesses concerning  

[10:4:66,549] the actions of state agencies, state laws and regulations adversely affecting those businesses. The OSBA maintains and  

[10:5:66,559] distributes an annual list of persons serving as small business ombudsmen throughout state government.  

[10:6:66,577] 0235010 - CALIFORNIA FILM COMMISSION  

[11:0:66,595] The California Film Commission (CFC) enhances California's status as the leader in motion picture, television and commercial  

[11:1:66,605] production. The Commission is tasked with retaining and increasing motion picture production and to see that it continues to  

[11:2:66,615] create jobs and boost business throughout the State. A one-stop office for filmmakers, the Commission supports productions of  

[11:3:66,625] all sizes and budgets with a variety of services. In addition to issuing film permits for all state properties, administering the film  

[11:4:66,636] and TV tax credit program, maintaining an extensive location library, and offering production assistance on a wide variety of  

[11:5:66,646] issues, CFC also works closely with cities and counties with the goal of creating 'film friendly" policies that are consistent state-

[11:6:66,656] wide.  

[12:0:66,674] 0235019 - DIVISION OF TOURISM  

[13:0:66,691] The California Tourism Market Act provides for the marketing of California through an assessment of businesses that benefit  

[13:1:66,702] from travel and tourism. The objective of the Tourism Assessment Program is to identify potentially assessable businesses,  

[13:2:66,712] assist companies with determining the appropriate amount of their self-assessment, and collet the fee.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:89,29] 0509   Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz) - 

[3:0:66,83] 0235028 - CALIFORINIA INFRASTRUCTURE AND ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT BANK  

[4:0:66,101] California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank) was created to finance public infrastructure and private  

[4:1:66,111] development that promote a healthy climate for jobs, contribute to a strong economy and improve the quality of life in California  

[4:2:66,121] communities. IBank has a broad authority to issue tax-exempt and taxable revenue bonds, provide financing to public  

[4:3:66,131] agencies, provide credit enhancements, acquire or lease facilities, and leverage State and Federal funds. IBank's current  

[4:4:66,141] programs include the infrastructure state revolving fund, 501(c)(3) tax-exempt and taxable revenue bond program, industrial  

[4:5:66,152] development revenue bond program, exempt facility revenue bond program, governmental bond program and the Clean  

[4:6:66,162] Energy Finance Center (CEFC) and the Statewide Energy Efficiency Program under the CEFC.  

[4:7:66,179] 0235037 - SMALL BUSINESS LOAN GUARANTEE PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,197] The Small Business Loan Guarantee Program (SBLGP) promotes local economic development by providing guarantees for  

[5:1:66,207] loans issued to small businesses from financial institutions, typically banks, that otherwise would not approve a term loan or  

[5:2:66,218] line of credit to a small business. As a result of the SBLGP, participating small businesses are able to secure financing that  

[5:3:66,228] allows them to grow and expand their business. The loan guarantee serves as a credit enhancement and an incentive for  

[5:4:66,238] financial institutions to make loans to small businesses that otherwise would not be eligible for such financing.  

[5:5:66,256] 0235046 - CALIFORNIA WELCOME CENTERS  

[6:0:66,273] California Welcome Centers are visitor information centers that are readily accessible to and recognizable by tourists to  

[6:1:66,284] encourage tourism in California and provide benefits to the state economy. The objective of the California Welcome Center  

[6:2:66,294] Program is to determine the locality of underserved travelers, designate a welcome center, and establish operating standards  

[6:3:66,304] across the network.  

[7:0:66,322] 0240 - COMMUNITY REINVESTMENT GRANT PROGRAM 

[8:0:66,339] The California Community Reinvestment Grants Program (CalCRG) was established on July 1, 2018   to   implement the  

[8:1:66,350] competitive grant program mandated by Proposition 64, the Adult Use of Marijuana Act, and the provisions specified in  

[8:2:66,360] Revenue and Taxation Code section 34019(d).   The CalCRG administers grants   for   local public   health departments and  

[8:3:66,370] qualified community-based nonprofit organizations to support various mental health treatment, substance use treatment, job  

[8:4:66,380] placement, legal services and other health and wellness-related programs for communities disproportionately affected by past  

[8:5:66,390] federal and state drug policies in California. GO-Biz must award grants annually and at least 50 percent of the grant funding will  

[8:6:66,401] be allocated to qualified community-based nonprofit organizations.

[3:0:66,83] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,102] 0270 - ADMINISTRATION OF TRANSPORTATION AGENCY  

[4:0:66,123] The Administration of the Transportation Agency, under direction of the Secretary, advises the Governor on major policy and  

[4:1:66,133] program matters and provides oversight and support of the Agency's departments and programs.  

[4:2:66,151] 0275 - CALIFORNIA TRAFFIC SAFETY PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,171] The California Traffic Safety Program develops the California Highway Safety Plan. The plan uses available state and federal  

[5:1:66,181] resources to identify and address major traffic safety problems throughout the state.  

[5:2:66,199] 0276 - TRANSIT AND INTERCITY RAIL CAPITAL PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,219] The Transit and Intercity Rail Capital Program provides grants to encourage low carbon transit operations through the Transit,  

[6:1:66,230] Affordable Housing, and Sustainable Communities Program. The program goals include reducing greenhouse gas emissions,  

[6:2:66,240] improving mobility access across the state, and providing benefits to California's disadvantaged communities.

[8:0:117,382] 0530   Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency  

[8:1:66,404] The primary mission of the Health and Human Services Agency (HHSA) is to provide policy leadership and direction to the  

[8:2:66,414] departments, board and programs it oversees, to reduce duplication and fragmentation among HHSA departments in policy  

[8:3:66,424] development and implementation, to improve coordination among departments on common programs, to ensure programmatic  

[8:4:66,434] integrity, and to advance the Governor's priorities on health and human services issues.  

[9:0:66,455] The HHSA accomplishes its mission through the administration and coordination of state and federal programs for public  

[9:1:66,465] health, health care services, social services, public assistance, health planning and licensing, and rehabilitation. These  

[9:2:66,475] programs touch the lives of millions of California's most needy and vulnerable residents. The HHSA is committed to striking a  

[9:3:66,485] balance between maintaining access to essential health and human services for California's most disadvantaged and at-risk  

[9:4:66,496] residents while pursuing ways to better manage and control costs.  

[10:0:66,516] The following departments and entities fall under the purview of the HHSA:  

[10:1:65,534] • Department of Aging  

[11:0:65,550] • Department of Child Support Services

[5:0:66,373] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,392] 0280 - SECRETARY FOR HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES  

[6:0:66,413] The Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency provides the Governor with the highest level of advice on  

[6:1:66,423] state health, human services and related budget policy issues. The Secretary also provides the highest level of leadership and  

[6:2:66,433] oversight of the agency-wide efforts to promote the health and well-being of a growing and increasingly diverse California  

[6:3:66,443] population.  

[7:0:66,461] 0285 - THE CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF HEALTH INFORMATION INTEGRITY  

[8:0:66,479] The California Office of Health Information Integrity (CalOHII) is responsible for statewide leadership, coordination, policy  

[8:1:66,489] formation, direction, and oversight responsibilities for federal Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA)  

[8:2:66,499] requirements. As the leading resource for providing HIPAA expertise to the State of California, CalOHII exercises full authority  

[8:3:66,509] relative to state entities to establish policy, provide direction to state entities, monitor progress and report on implementation  

[8:4:66,520] efforts based on state and federal laws.  

[8:5:66,537] 0290 - OFFICE OF SYSTEMS INTEGRATION  

[9:0:66,558] The Office of Systems Integration's (OSI) mission is to procure, manage, and deliver complex technology systems that support  

[9:1:66,568] the delivery of health and human services for automation projects for the Health and Human Services Agency, the Department  

[9:2:66,578] of Social Services, the Department of Health Care Services, the Department of Public Health, and the Health Benefit  

[9:3:66,588] Exchange, including: 

[9:4:65,606] • Appeals Case Management System  

[10:0:65,622] • California Healthcare Eligibility, Enrollment and Retention System (CalHEERS)  

[10:1:65,638] • Case Management, Information and Payrolling System II (CMIPS)  

[10:2:65,655] • Child Welfare Services/Case Management System (CWS/CMS)  

[10:3:65,671] • Child Welfare Digital Services (CWDS)  

[11:0:65,687] • Child Welfare Services - California Automated Response and Engagement System  

[11:1:65,703] • Electronic Benefit Transfer System (EBT)

[11:0:164,501] 0540   Secretary of the Natural Resources Agency  

[12:0:66,522] The mission of the Natural Resources Agency is to restore, protect and manage the state's natural, historical and cultural  

[12:1:66,533] resources for current and future generations using creative approaches and solutions based on science, collaboration and  

[12:2:66,543] respect for all involved communities. The secretary for Natural Resources, a member of the Governor's Cabinet, sets the  

[12:3:66,553] policies and coordinates the environmental preservation and restoration activities of 26 various departments, boards,  

[12:4:66,563] commissions and conservancies, and directly administers the Sea Grant Program, Ocean Protection Council, California  

[12:5:66,573] Environmental Quality Act, Environmental Enhancement Mitigation Program, River Parkways, Urban Greening, and the  

[12:6:66,584] California Cultural and Historical Endowment grant programs.  

[13:0:66,604] The Natural Resources Agency consists of the departments of Forestry and Fire Protection, Conservation, Fish and Wildlife,  

[13:1:66,614] Parks and Recreation, and Water Resources; the California Conservation Corps; Exposition Park; California Science Center;  

[13:2:66,624] California African American Museum; the State Lands Commission; the Colorado River Board; the San Francisco Bay  

[13:3:66,635] Conservation and Development Commission; the Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission; the Wildlife  

[13:4:66,645] Conservation Board; the Delta Protection Commission; the California Coastal Commission; the State Coastal Conservancy; the  

[13:5:66,655] California Tahoe Conservancy; the Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy; the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy; the  

[13:6:66,665] San Joaquin River Conservancy; the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy; the Baldwin  

[13:7:66,675] Hills Conservancy; the San Diego River Conservancy; the Sierra Nevada Conservancy; the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta  

[13:8:66,686] Conservancy; the Native American Heritage Commission; and the Special Resources Program.

[5:0:66,534] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:1:66,553] The California Natural Resources Agency has one Capital Outlay project, overseeing the Species Conservation Habitat Project  

[5:2:66,564] at the Salton Sea. The Project includes development of a series of ponds, sediment mixing basins, ditches, and berms allowing  

[5:3:66,574] water currently detached from the sea to be managed to provide suitable habitat for fish and birds. The Project is one  

[5:4:66,584] component of the Salton Sea Management Plan.

[7:0:199,293] 0552   Office of the Inspector General  

[8:0:66,314] The Office of the Inspector General (OIG) protects public safety by safeguarding the integrity of California's correctional  

[8:1:66,324] system. OIG is responsible for contemporaneous oversight of the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation's  

[8:2:66,334] (CDCR) internal affairs investigations, use of force, staff complaints, and the employee disciplinary process. The Inspector  

[8:3:66,345] General audits and reviews the policies, practices, and procedures of CDCR. The Inspector General reviews the Governor's  

[8:4:66,355] candidates for appointment to serve as warden for the state's adult correctional institutions and as superintendents for the  

[8:5:66,365] state's juvenile facilities, and conducts metric-oriented inspection programs to periodically review delivery of medical care at  

[8:6:66,375] each state prison. OIG receives communications from individuals alleging improper governmental activity and maintains a toll-

[8:7:66,385] free public telephone number to receive allegations of wrongdoing by employees of CDCR; conducts formal reviews of  

[8:8:66,396] complaints of retaliation from CDCR employees against upper management where a legally cognizable cause of action is  

[8:9:66,406] present; and reviews sexual abuse incidents within correctional institutions. OIG provides critical public transparency for the  

[8:10:66,416] state correctional system by publicly reporting its findings.  

[9:0:66,434] In addition, OIG is responsible for the California Rehabilitation Oversight Board (Board) that examines CDCR's various mental  

[9:1:66,444] health, substance abuse, educational, and employment programs for inmates and parolees. The Board meets at least twice  

[9:2:66,454] annually to recommend modifications, additions, and eliminations of offender rehabilitation and treatment programs. The Board  

[9:3:66,464] also submits biannual reports to the Governor, the Legislature, and the public to convey its findings on the effectiveness of  

[9:4:66,474] treatment efforts, rehabilitation needs of offenders, gaps in offender rehabilitation services, and levels of offender participation  

[9:5:66,485] and success.

[9:0:175,431] 0555   Secretary for Environmental Protection  

[10:0:66,453] The Secretary for Environmental Protection is the cabinet-level agency responsible for protecting the environment. California  

[10:1:66,463] Environmental Protection Agency programs reduce greenhouse gas emissions, restore and protect environmental quality, and  

[10:2:66,473] protect public health. The Secretary coordinates the state's environmental regulatory programs and ensures fair and consistent  

[10:3:66,483] enforcement of environmental law, which safeguards the state's residents and promotes the state's economic vitality. The  

[10:4:66,494] Secretary also administers environmental justice, environmental law enforcement, emergency preparedness and response,  

[10:5:66,504] Certified Unified Program Agencies, and scientific review programs. The Secretary leads greenhouse gas emission reduction  

[10:6:66,514] and climate change activities in state government, and is responsible for coordinating the implementation of Chapter 488,  

[10:7:66,524] Statutes of 2006 (AB 32). The Secretary also centralizes coordination of California-Mexico border environmental efforts.

[6:1:66,250] The Labor and Workforce Development Agency was established to address issues relating to California workers and their  

[6:2:66,260] employers. The Agency is responsible for labor law enforcement, workforce development, and benefit payment and  

[6:3:66,270] adjudication. The Agency works to combat the underground economy and help legitimate businesses and workers in California  

[6:4:66,280] through a combination of enforcement and education activities.

[6:0:195,286] 0650   Office of Planning and Research  

[7:0:66,307] The Office of Planning and Research (OPR) assists the Governor and the Administration in planning, research, policy  

[7:1:66,317] development, and legislative analysis. OPR formulates long-range state goals and policies to address land use, climate  

[7:2:66,328] change, population growth and distribution, urban expansion, infrastructure development, groundwater sustainability and  

[7:3:66,338] drought response, and resource protection. OPR also coordinates guidelines and compliance of the California Environmental  

[7:4:66,348] Quality Act (CEQA). OPR acts as the state's liaison to a variety of entities including local government, planning professionals,  

[7:5:66,358] small business, and the Department of Defense.  

[8:0:66,376] OPR houses and supports the Strategic Growth Council, a program created to coordinate state agency activities in supporting  

[8:1:66,386] planning and development of sustainable communities and California Volunteers, a program aimed to increase the number and  

[8:2:66,396] impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering throughout the state.

[5:0:66,244] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,264] 0360 - STATE PLANNING AND POLICY DEVELOPMENT  

[7:0:66,281] By statute, the office is the state’s comprehensive planning agency. The major activities of the State Planning and Policy  

[7:1:66,292] Development program include: (1) recommending and implementing state policies on land-use and growth planning and  

[7:2:66,302] compliance with environmental statutes and regulations, including General Plan Guidelines and other guidance to local and  

[7:3:66,312] regional governments; (2) coordinating the implementation of the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), including: (a)  

[7:4:66,322] operating the State Clearinghouse; (b) maintaining an online database for the electronic submission and archiving of CEQA  

[7:5:66,332] documents; (c) developing CEQA guidelines amendments; (d) providing technical assistance; and (e) providing training; (3)  

[7:6:66,343] supporting the Governor's Advisor for Military Affairs and Governor's Military Council; (4) providing policy research for the  

[7:7:66,353] Governor and Cabinet; (5) supporting local planning efforts related to the impacts of droughts and wildfires;(6) supporting  

[7:8:66,363] development of state plans including, but not limited to, the Water Action Plan, the State Multi-Hazard Mitigation Plan, the  

[7:9:66,373] Safeguarding California Plan, the Integrated Energy Policy Report, and the 5-Year Infrastructure Plan; (7) supporting multiple  

[7:10:66,383] efforts to address climate change and response to carbon pollution; (8) administering the Integrated Climate Adaptation and  

[7:11:66,394] Resiliency Program to coordinate regional and local efforts with state climate adaptation strategies to adapt to the impacts of  

[7:12:66,404] climate change; (9) housing the Federal Grants Administrator for tracking of federal grants coming to California and providing  

[7:13:66,414] information on federal grants to help maximize federal grant opportunities within the state; (10) administering the California  

[7:14:66,424] Education Learning Lab,   California Initiative to Advance Precision Medicine, the Cradle-to-Career, and Higher Education  

[7:15:66,434] Innovation grant programs; (11) Establishing and facilitating the Rural Economic Development Steering Committee/Wood  

[7:16:66,445] Utilization Work Group to promote rural economic development that drives healthy and sustainable forest management  

[7:17:66,455] consistent with our rural economic development,   public health and safety, and climate goals; and (13) conducting other

[8:0:66,483] 0365 - CALIFORNIA VOLUNTEERS  

[9:0:66,500] California Volunteers is charged with increasing the number and impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering  

[9:1:66,511] throughout the state. California Volunteers administers programs such as AmeriCorps and Disaster Volunteering and  

[10:0:66,521] Preparedness, and guides policy development to support the nonprofit and service fields.   Through the efforts of California  

[10:1:66,531] Volunteers, Californians of all ages and abilities are provided ongoing opportunities to volunteer, to become better prepared to  

[10:2:66,541] respond in emergencies and disasters, and ultimately, to embrace a lifetime ethic of service.  

[11:0:66,559] 0370 - STRATEGIC GROWTH COUNCIL  

[12:0:66,577] The California Strategic Growth Council (SGC) coordinates and works collaboratively with public agencies, communities, and  

[12:1:66,587] stakeholders to promote sustainable community development, preserve natural resources, reduce greenhouse gas emissions,  

[12:2:66,597] advance equity, and protect public health. SGC administers the following grant programs: Affordable Housing and Sustainable  

[12:3:66,607] Communities; Transformative Climate Communities; Sustainable Agricultural Lands Conservation; and Climate Change  

[12:4:66,617] Research. Each of these programs contributes to greenhouse gas emission reductions and is part of the California Climate  

[13:0:66,628] Investments program, funded by proceeds from the state’s cap and trade auction revenues. For each program, SGC works with  

[13:1:66,638] an interagency team to develop program guidelines, review applications, and administer program funds. SGC also administers  

[13:2:66,648] the Regional Climate Collaboratives, which supports local capacity building in under-resourced communities across the state.  

[13:3:66,658] SGC’s Technical Assistance program supports access to SGC’s own grant programs as well as those of many other state  

[13:4:66,668] agencies. This technical assistance improves the accessibility of these programs and advances projects that maximize  

[13:5:66,679] greenhouse gas reductions. SGC also houses two collaborative policy initiatives: the Health in All Policies program, which  

[13:6:66,689] advances integration of health and equity principles in existing state programs and planning; and a partnership with the High  

[13:7:66,699] Speed Rail authority to support sustainable development and conservation practices through that project. SGC is also charged  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[12:1:66,695] support our communities for a resilient California. The Cal OES Director serves as both the Governor's Homeland Security  

[12:2:66,705] Advisor and Emergency Management Director, with responsibility to implement and facilitate the state's homeland security and

[0:0:66,13] 2020-21 STATE BUDGET — LJE   110 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:167,29] 0690   Office of Emergency Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,83] counter-terrorism strategy. Cal OES serves the public through effective collaboration in preparing for, protecting against,  

[3:1:66,93] responding to, recovering from, and mitigating the impacts of hazards and threats.  

[4:0:66,111] Cal OES provides leadership, assistance, training, and support to state and local agencies and coordinates with federal  

[4:1:66,121] agencies in planning and preparing for the most effective use of federal, state, local, and private sector resources in  

[4:2:66,131] emergencies. This emergency planning is based upon a system of mutual aid whereby a jurisdiction relies first on its own  

[4:3:66,141] resources, and then requests assistance from its neighbors. Cal OES' plans and programs are coordinated with those of the  

[4:4:66,152] federal government, other states, private sector, utilities, and state and local agencies within California.  

[5:0:66,169] During an emergency, Cal OES functions as the Governor's immediate staff to provide guidance and coordinate the state's  

[5:1:66,179] responsibilities under the Emergency Services Act and applicable federal statutes. It also acts as the conduit for federal  

[5:2:66,190] assistance through natural disaster grants and federal agency support. Additionally, Cal OES is responsible for the  

[5:3:66,200] development and coordination of a comprehensive state strategy related to all hazards, including terrorism, which includes  

[5:4:66,210] prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery.  

[6:0:66,228] Cal OES implements the state's homeland security strategy by overseeing the California Cybersecurity Integration Center and  

[6:1:66,238] the State Threat Assessment System (STAS), which includes management of the State Threat Assessment Center. The STAS  

[6:2:66,248] assists in the detection, prevention, and investigation of   and response to criminal and terrorist activity and   also facilitates  

[6:3:66,258] information and intelligence sharing between local, state, federal, tribal agencies, and private sector partners.  

[6:4:66,276] The Budget reflects the transfer of the Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety   Commission to the Office of Emergency Services  

[6:5:66,286] beginning in 2020-21.  

[7:0:66,304] Because Cal OES' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Cal OES has a capital outlay program to support this  

[7:1:66,314] need. For the specifics on Cal OES' capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[8:0:66,335] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[9:0:65,354] • Community Power Resiliency—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund for additional preparedness  

[9:1:76,364] measures to bolster community resiliency. Building on the state’s 2019-20 power-resiliency investments, critical services still  

[9:2:76,374] vulnerable to power outage events include schools, county election offices, and food storage reserves.  

[10:0:65,390] • California Earthquake Early Warning System—The Budget includes $17.3 million California Earthquake Safety Fund in  

[10:1:76,401] 2020-21, which relies on a one-time $17.3 million School Land Bank Fund loan while ongoing revenue options continue to  

[10:2:76,411] be evaluated and pursued. These funds will support the system operations and program management, an education and  

[10:3:76,421] outreach campaign, and research and development to expand earthquake mitigation uses.  

[11:0:65,437] • California Cybersecurity Integration Center—The Budget includes three-year limited-term funding of $7.6   million General  

[11:1:76,447] Fund in 2020-21 and $8.1   million in 2021-22 and 2022-23 to support additional capacity within the California Cybersecurity  

[11:2:76,458] Integration Center, consistent with the requirements of Government Code section   8586.5. This funding is part of a  

[11:3:76,468] collaborative effort between the Department of Technology, Office of Emergency Services, California Military Department,  

[11:4:76,478] and the California Highway Patrol.  

[12:0:65,494] • Wildfire Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center (SB 209)—The Budget includes $2 million ongoing General  

[12:1:76,504] Fund for OES—in conjunction with the Department of Forestry and Fire Protection, California Public Utilities Commission,  

[12:2:76,515] and California Military Department—to establish and operate the Wildfire Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center,  

[12:3:76,525] consistent with the requirements of Chapter 405, Statutes of 2019 (SB 209).  

[13:0:65,541] • Transfer of the Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety Commission to the Office of Emergency Services—The Budget reflects the  

[13:1:76,551] transfer of the Commission, its six positions, and associated funding to Cal OES beginning in 2020-21. To support this  

[13:2:76,561] transfer, the Budget includes $2.5   million ($503,000 General Fund) in 2020-21 and $2.4   million ongoing ($351,000 General  

[13:3:76,572] Fund). As part of the state’s broader preparedness efforts, the Commission will be better positioned to provide a coordinated  

[13:4:76,582] framework for establishing earthquake safety policies and recommendations, and tracking the state’s progress toward higher  

[13:5:76,592] levels of seismic safety. The Budget also proposes a reduction in the number of appointed Commissioners from 20 to 15 and  

[13:6:76,602] an expansion of the entities required to provide updates to the Commission on earthquake preparedness and seismic safety  

[13:7:76,612] activities.

[3:0:66,53] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,72] 0380 - EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT SERVICES  

[5:0:66,93] Working through the state's mutual aid system, the Emergency Management Services and Homeland Security Program  

[5:1:66,103] coordinates the provision   of federal, state, and local resources to jurisdictions whose resources and services are overextended  

[5:2:66,113] in a disaster situation. In addition, this program develops and coordinates a comprehensive state strategy related to an all-

[5:3:66,123] hazards approach that includes prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery consistent with the National  

[5:4:66,134] Strategy on Homeland Security. Central to this effort is maintaining operational readiness at all levels of government. Within this  

[5:5:66,144] program, Cal OES also trains emergency managers, responders, and state and local leaders in preparedness and response to  

[5:6:66,154] all hazards.  

[6:0:66,172] 0385 - SPECIAL PROGRAMS AND GRANT MANAGEMENT  

[7:0:66,189] Cal OES serves as the State Administering Agent for federal homeland security, emergency management, and victim services  

[7:1:66,200] grants and is the primary liaison with the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the United States Department of Homeland  

[7:2:66,210] Security, and other federal agencies. Additionally, Cal OES serves as an advocate for local public safety, emergency  

[7:3:66,220] management, and public and private agencies operating public safety and victim service programs. As part of its grant  

[7:4:66,230] management role, Cal OES administers the California Disaster Assistance Act and provides eligible local agencies with  

[7:5:66,240] financial assistance to recover from disasters. Included in this program is the State Threat Assessment Center, which is the  

[7:6:66,251] centerpiece of the state's intelligence information sharing environment and serves as the Governor's designated primary fusion  

[7:7:66,261] center.  

[8:0:66,278] 0390 - ALFRED E. ALQUIST SEISMIC SAFETY COMMISSION  

[9:0:66,296] The Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety Commission program provides a coordinated framework for establishing consistent  

[9:1:66,306] earthquake policies; advising the Governor, the Legislature, local governments, and the public; and tracking the state’s  

[9:2:66,317] progress toward higher levels of seismic safety.   The Commission uses the expertise of its commissioners to review, evaluate,  

[9:3:66,327] and translate scientific information and make recommendations to guide and influence earthquake safety policies.   The  

[9:4:66,337] Commission works with federal, state, and local agencies, as well as the private sector, on a variety of activities that support  

[9:5:66,347] the state’s earthquake preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery.   These activities include: (1) developing and  

[9:6:66,357] reviewing seismic safety projects; (2) providing consistent policy direction; (3) proposing and reviewing earthquake-related  

[9:7:66,368] legislation; (4) conducting public hearings on seismic safety issues; (5) using existing knowledge and conducting studies,  

[9:8:66,378] where necessary, to improve the performance of structures in California, (6) recommending earthquake safety programs and  

[9:9:66,388] supporting cost-effective partnerships that help reduce earthquake risks and speed economic recovery.  

[9:10:66,406] 0395 - PUBLIC SAFETY COMMUNICATIONS  

[10:0:66,426] The Public Safety Communications   program is responsible for the design, installation, maintenance, and repair of the  

[10:1:66,436] state's   public safety communications networks and oversight of the state's 9-1-1 system. The   program is dedicated to the  

[10:2:66,446] preservation and protection of human life and public safety by delivering reliable and dependable communication services that  

[10:3:66,457] keep the public connected during times of crisis.  

[10:4:66,474] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND EXECUTIVE  

[11:0:66,495] The Administration and Executive Program provides the overall policy direction of   Cal OES and support services such as  

[11:1:66,505] accounting, fiscal, personnel, information technology, and business services.

[4:0:66,145] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:0:66,165] The Office of Emergency Services' infrastructure includes a headquarters facility, an administrative building, and an Inland  

[5:1:66,175] Regional Operations Center in Sacramento County; a Coastal Regional Operations Center in Fairfield in Solano   County; a  

[5:2:66,185] Southern Regional Operations Center located at Los Alamitos Joint Forces Training Base in Orange County; the California  

[5:3:66,195] Specialized Training Institute at Camp San Luis Obispo; and various small field offices throughout the state.  

[6:0:66,216] The Public Safety Communications division (PSC) is comprised of a main leased complex in Sacramento and 45 field locations  

[6:1:66,226] throughout the state. These locations include 8 area offices and 37 area shops, positioned geographically to facilitate  

[6:2:66,236] maintenance and installation services to remote communication sites and customers throughout the state. In addition, the PSC  

[6:3:66,246] maintains and operates 11 communications vaults/towers and a total of more than 3,500 radio frequency points of presence.

[2:0:175,135] 0730   Governor Elect and Outgoing Governor  

[2:1:66,157] These funds are to provide assistance to the Governor-elect and the outgoing Governor, during the transition period following  

[2:2:66,167] the election, in carrying out the duties described under the provisions of Government Code Sections 12015 and 12015.5.  

[3:0:66,187] The law provides that state agencies furnish to the Governor-elect any information or assistance necessary in the preparation  

[3:1:66,197] of the annual state budget and for the orderly transfer of the executive power. This gives the Governor-elect approximately two  

[3:2:66,208] months to formulate policies and to incorporate them in the various programs included in the Budget, which will be transmitted  

[3:3:66,218] to the Legislature.  

[4:0:66,238] The law also provides for the outgoing Governor to appoint persons for up to a two-month period, to assist in concluding  

[4:1:66,248] matters arising from official duties during the last term of office.

[7:0:191,354] 0750   Office of the Lieutenant Governor  

[7:1:66,376] Under California's Constitution, the Lieutenant Governor serves as Acting Governor whenever the Governor is absent from the  

[7:2:66,386] state, and automatically becomes Governor if a vacancy occurs in the Office of Governor. The Lieutenant Governor is also  

[7:3:66,396] President of the Senate and votes in case of a tie.  

[8:0:66,416] The Lieutenant Governor serves as a voting member of the Board of Regents of the University of California and a voting  

[8:1:66,427] member of the Board of Trustees of the California State University system.  

[9:0:66,447] The Lieutenant Governor also serves on, and rotates with the State Controller, as chair of the three-member State Lands  

[9:1:66,457] Commission, which oversees the control and leasing of millions of acres of state-owned land, including offshore oil resources,  

[9:2:66,467] as well as use and permitting for all navigable waterways in California. The Commission also manages state land-use planning  

[9:3:66,478] and revenues, and related interstate issues. During alternate years, when the Lieutenant Governor serves as Chairperson of  

[9:4:66,488] the State Lands Commission, the Lieutenant Governor also serves as a member of the Ocean Protection Council   and as a  

[9:5:66,498] non-voting member of the California Coastal Commission.  

[10:0:66,518] In addition, under state statutes, the Lieutenant Governor chairs the California Commission for Economic Development, which  

[10:1:66,529] provides support and guidance to the Governor, Legislature, and private sector regarding the development of California's  

[10:2:66,539] economy.

[11:0:224,509] 0820   Department of Justice  

[12:0:66,530] The constitutional office of the Attorney General, as chief law officer of the state, has the responsibility to see that the laws of  

[12:1:66,540] California are uniformly and adequately enforced. This responsibility is fulfilled through the diverse programs of the Department  

[12:2:66,551] of Justice.  

[13:0:66,571] The Department of Justice is responsible for providing skillful and efficient legal services on behalf of the people of California.  

[13:1:66,581] The Attorney General represents the people in all matters before the Appellate and Supreme Courts of California and the  

[13:2:66,591] United States; serves as legal counsel to state officers, boards, commissioners, and departments; represents the people in  

[13:3:66,602] actions to protect the environment and to enforce consumer, antitrust, and civil laws; and assists district attorneys in the  

[13:4:66,612] administration of justice. The Department provides oversight, enforcement, education, and regulation of California's firearms/ 

[13:5:66,622] dangerous weapon laws; provides evaluation and analysis of physical evidence; regulates legal gambling activities in  

[13:6:66,632] California; supports the telecommunications and data processing needs of the California criminal justice community; and  

[13:7:66,642] pursues projects designed to protect the people of California from fraudulent, unfair, and illegal activities.  

[14:0:66,660] Because the Department of Justice's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay  

[14:1:66,670] program to support this need.   For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[13:0:66,375] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[14:0:65,394] • Bureau of Forensic Services Backfill—The Budget includes $35.8 million one-time ($25.8 million General Fund and $10  

[14:1:76,404] million Fingerprint Fees Account) to backfill the continued decline in fine and fee revenues in the DNA Identification Fund.  

[15:0:65,421] • Erskine Fire Claims Settlement—The Budget includes $11.6 million one-time General Fund to pay for claims related to the  

[15:1:76,431] Erskine Wildfire.  

[16:0:65,447] • Healthcare Rights and Access Section—The Budget includes $6.9 million in 2020-21 ($3.7 million Attorney General Antitrust  

[16:1:76,457] Account and $3.2 million Unfair Competition Law Fund) and $6.7 million annually thereafter ($3.6 million Attorney General  

[16:2:76,467] Antitrust Account and $3.1 million Unfair Competition Law fund) to establish the Healthcare Rights and Access Section to  

[16:3:76,478] consolidate and centralize healthcare litigation within the Department of Justice.  

[17:0:65,494] • Firearms: Precursor Parts (Chapter 730, Statutes of 2019 (AB 879))—The Budget includes $5.9 million General Fund in  

[17:1:76,504] 2020-21 and $8.3 million in 2021-22 to regulate and track the sale of firearm precursor parts. Given the risk that such guns  

[17:2:76,514] present to public safety, the Budget includes resources to accelerate the implementation of these requirements to July 1,  

[17:3:76,524] 2022.  

[18:0:65,541] • Criminal Records: Automatic Relief (Chapter 578, Statutes of 2019 (AB 1076))—The Budget includes $3.7 million Fingerprint  

[18:1:76,551] Fees Account in 2020-21, $4 million in 2021-22, and $1.9 million annually thereafter to review records in statewide criminal  

[18:2:76,561] justice databases to identify persons eligible to have their arrest or criminal conviction records withheld from disclosure and  

[18:3:76,571] modify existing systems to grant such relief automatically.  

[19:0:65,587] • Tax Recovery in the Underground Economy (TRUE) Criminal Enforcement Program—The Budget includes $3.3 million  

[19:1:76,598] General Fund in 2020-21 and $3 million annually thereafter to bolster one of the existing TRUE teams.  

[20:0:65,614] • California Law Enforcement Telecommunications System: Immigration (Chapter 789, Statutes of 2019 (AB 1747))—The  

[20:1:76,624] Budget includes $2.8 million General Fund in 2020-21, $2.9 million in 2021-22, and $2.5 million annually thereafter to  

[20:2:76,634] conduct investigations and audits to monitor compliance with AB 1747, which limits the use of the California Law  

[20:3:76,644] Enforcement Telecommunications System for immigration enforcement purposes.  

[21:0:65,661] • Firearms Information Technology Systems Modernization—The Budget includes $2.4 million one-time Dealers Record of  

[21:1:76,671] Sale Special Account for the initial planning and analysis phase of a new firearms system that will combine 11 existing  

[21:2:76,681] firearms systems into one comprehensive system. The current systems are antiquated, costly to update, and inefficient at  

[21:3:76,691] gathering data.

[5:0:66,194] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,213] 0435 - LEGAL SERVICES  

[7:0:66,234] Legal Services is organized into three elements: (1) Civil Law, (2) Criminal Law, and (3) Public Rights.  

[8:0:66,254] Civil Law represents the State of California and its officers, agencies, departments, boards, commissions, and employees in  

[8:1:66,264] civil matters. It provides advice to these clients, defends cases brought against them, and prosecutes cases to vindicate state  

[8:2:66,275] interests. Deputy Attorneys General in Civil Law are responsible for managing and litigating cases before administrative  

[8:3:66,285] tribunals, and in both state and federal courts at the trial level and on appeal, including appeals before the United States and  

[8:4:66,295] California Supreme Courts. Deputies work in one of   nine sections: Business and Tax; Cannabis Control; Correctional Law;  

[8:5:66,305] Employment and Administrative Mandates; Government Law; Health, Education, and Welfare; Health Quality Enforcement;  

[8:6:66,315] Licensing; or Tort and Condemnation.  

[9:0:66,336] Criminal Law represents the   People of the State of California   in criminal matters before the Appellate and Supreme courts.  

[9:1:66,346] Criminal Law also fulfills the Attorney General's responsibilities of assisting district attorneys in cases for which they are  

[9:2:66,356] recused, conducting criminal investigations, representing the Governor, Board of Parole Hearings, and California Department of  

[9:3:66,366] Corrections and Rehabilitation in state and federal habeas corpus cases and appeals, and other proceedings relating to parole  

[9:4:66,377] decisions and conditions of confinement in the state prisons, and defending criminal judgements in state and federal habeas  

[9:5:66,387] corpus matters. Additional responsibilities include enforcing the Political Reform Act, advising the Governor on extradition  

[9:6:66,397] matters, investigating and prosecuting Medi-Cal provider fraud, investigating and prosecuting the abuse or neglect of elder and  

[9:7:66,407] dependent adults residing in long-term health care facilities, and investigating, prosecuting, and coordinating litigation involving  

[9:8:66,417] white-collar crimes, high-tech/computer/privacy crimes, financial crimes against the elderly, human trafficking, environmental  

[9:9:66,428] crimes, and public corruption.  

[10:0:66,448] Public Rights protects and preserves the public’s interests by providing legal services to state agencies and Constitutional  

[10:1:66,458] Officers and by bringing actions in the name of the Attorney General to protect the public. Public Rights provides specialized  

[10:2:66,468] services in the following areas: Civil Rights Enforcement (including Worker’s Rights and Fair Labor Bureau and the Bureau of  

[10:3:66,479] Children's Justice); Charitable Trusts (including the Registry of Charitable Trusts); Natural Resources Law; Corporate Fraud  

[10:4:66,489] (including False Claims, Energy and Corporate Responsibility); Healthcare Rights and Access; Indian and Gaming Law;  

[10:5:66,499] Environment Law (including the Bureau of Environmental Justice); Land Law; Consumer Law (including Sellers of Travel and  

[10:6:66,509] Privacy Enforcement and Protection); Antitrust Law; and Tobacco Litigation and Enforcement.  

[11:0:66,527] 0440 - LAW ENFORCEMENT  

[12:0:66,547] The Division of Law Enforcement is organized into five elements: (1) Bureau of Firearms, (2) Bureau of Forensic Services, (3)  

[12:1:66,557] Bureau of Gambling Control, (4) Bureau of Investigation, and (5) the Office of the Chief.  

[13:0:66,575] The Bureau of Firearms provides oversight, enforcement, education, and regulation of California's firearms and ammunitions  

[13:1:66,585] laws by administering 35 different state-mandated firearms and ammunition-related programs. It is accomplished by conducting  

[13:2:66,596] firearm and ammunition eligibility checks, conducting compliance inspections at firearm dealers, ammunition vendors, and  

[13:3:66,606] manufacturers, and by providing valuable training to stakeholders statewide. Special Agents conduct investigations on armed  

[13:4:66,616] and prohibited persons and other investigations resulting in the seizure of firearms, magazines, and ammunition. Agents also  

[13:5:66,626] conduct investigations to monitor and prevent illegal firearm trafficking at permitted gun shows in accordance with state and  

[13:6:66,636] federal laws.  

[14:0:66,654] The Bureau of Forensic Services (BFS) provides evaluation and analysis of physical evidence, including crime scene  

[14:1:66,664] investigation and expert court testimony to federal, state and local law enforcement agencies, district attorneys, and courts, by  

[14:2:66,674] operating 11 specialized laboratories that serve 46 counties as well as a forensic training facility. BFS maintains the state DNA  

[14:3:66,685] laboratory database, which compiles DNA profiles of sex and violent offenders and felony arrestees.  

[15:0:66,702] The Bureau of Gambling Control regulates legal gambling activities in California to ensure gambling is conducted honestly and  

[15:1:66,713] is free from criminal and corruptive elements. This is accomplished by investigating the qualifications of individuals and  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.
